Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0010.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Nanostructured black anatase titania with oxygen vacancies (OVs) is efficiently obtained and employed as an anode in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) for the first time. The incorporation of OVs into TiO<sub>2</sub> is demonstrated to render considerably enhanced-rate performances, higher initial capacities, and an accelerated electrochemical activation process during cycling, derived from the boosted intrinsic electric conductivity and improved kinetics of Na uptake. Bestowed with the integrated merits of OVs and shortened Na ion diffusion length in the nanostructure, black titania delivers a reversible specific capacity of 207.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 C, retains 99.1% over 500 cycles at 1 C stably, and still maintains 91.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even at the high rate of 20 C. Density functional theory (DFT) calculations suggest that the lower sodiation energy barrier of anatase with OVs enables a more favorable Na intercalation into black anatase. Thus, it is of great significance to introduce OVs into TiO<sub>2</sub> to stimulate ultrafast and durable sodium-storage properties, which also offers a potential strategy to project more superior electrodes, utilizing internal defects.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D9142%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DJun%2BChen%252C%2BZhiying%2BDing%252C%2BChao%2BWang%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D8%26issueNum%3D14%26contentID%3Dacsami.6b01183%26title%3DBlack%2BAnatase%2BTitania%2Bwith%2BUltrafast%2BSodium-Storage%2BPerformances%2BStimulated%2Bby%2BOxygen%2BVacancies%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D9151%26publicationDate%3DApril%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290834&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Defects in solids" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Defects%20in%20solids" class="article__tags__link">Defects in solids</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290667&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Ions" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Ions" class="article__tags__link">Ions</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291999&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Minerals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Minerals" class="article__tags__link">Minerals</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290689&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Oxides" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Oxides" class="article__tags__link">Oxides</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291251&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Sodium" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Sodium" class="article__tags__link">Sodium</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Black+Anatase&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_black%20anatase">black anatase</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Oxygen+Vacancies&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_oxygen%20vacancies">oxygen vacancies</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Rate+Performances&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_rate%20performances">rate performances</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">1 Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">On account of the natural abundance of sodium reserves, lower production cost and comparable electrochemical performances compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), current years have witnessed an exceedingly blooming trend in the research of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which are hopeful substitutes for LIBs in certain areas, especially for large-scale applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1-3)</a> Accordingly, exploiting suitable electrode materials for sodium storages becomes highly crucial. In regard to the anode, hard carbonaceous materials have been proven to possess reasonable sodium storage capabilities, whose inherent physical properties and random structure, however, could seriously hamper Na insertion at high rates. Alternatively, the alloy-type-based materials such as Sn, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Sb <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a>, and P <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> together with the conversion-type-based anodes like sulfides <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> have been demonstrated to exhibit high initial capacity, whereas suffer from poor cyclability mostly caused by the huge volume change. As a type of intercalation-based anode materials, TiO<sub>2</sub> of various polymorphs (including anatase, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8-10)</a> rutile, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11-13)</a> and TiO<sub>2</sub> (B) <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14, 15)</a> as well as amorphous titania <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16, 17)</a>) have recently aroused increasing scientific enthusiasm for sodium storage owing to their superior qualities of being nonpoisononous, having natural abundance and low cost, and especially having an excellent cycling stability, conferring with their plentiful investigations in LIBs systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18, 19)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Among polyphase titania, anatase has been considered to be more electrochemically active for SIBs than rutile or amorphous titania; the suitable diffusion pathways of two dimensions and more plentiful accommodation sites for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation could be provided by the anatase crystal structure, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> which is also supported by the theoretical study revealing a relatively low activation barrier of sodium insertion into the anatase lattice. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 20 to 22">(20-22)</a> However, the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into anatase is still kinetically hindered, especially at high rates, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> which could be reflected from the activation phenomenon in the discharge–charge process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24, 25)</a> Moreover, the quite poor electronic conductivity (∼10<sup>–13</sup> S cm<sup>–1</sup>) makes TiO<sub>2</sub> a semiconductor derived from the huge band gap of ∼3.2 eV, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> which severely leads to its inferior rate capabilities. Targeting these main inherent shortcomings, relevant research efforts have been devoted. One typical solution is to improve the diffusion of Na ions by means of nanostructure construction, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 25 and 27 to 29">(25, 27-29)</a> and the other is aimed at the amelioration of electron transfer through conductive carbon supporting <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 30 to 33">(30-33)</a> and doping with alien elements, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33-35)</a> among which the doping one is considerably appealing because the electronic conductivity of TiO<sub>2</sub> itself could be advanced; in addition, the structure defects would hopefully provide more open channels for ion transport, as has been shown in LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> Nevertheless, it should be noted that the thermal stability might be declined by heterogeneous defects of impurity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Another type of homogeneous defects, oxygen vacancies, which can alter the electronic structures as well as the energetics of ions and electrons transportation in metal oxides without sacrificing crystal stability, have been proven to effectively enable better MoO<sub>3–<i>x</i></sub> sodium-ion battery performances, as shown by Xu et al. very recently. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Triggered by that, the oxygen vacancies induced Ti<sup>3+</sup> self-doping, which usually endows TiO<sub>2</sub> with a black color, should be attached enough attention to enhance the sodium storage performances of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, while to the best of our knowledge, relevant research is currently absent. As reported anteriorly, the band gap of black titania could be remarkably narrowed to ∼1.5 eV and therefore boost the intrinsic electronic conductivity greatly, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39-41)</a> which is promising for the improvement of durable rate capabilities referring to our previous work on Ti<sup>3+</sup> self-doped titania for LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Furthermore, the introduction of oxygen vacancies can slightly tailor the anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> host structure, which might reasonably generate certain effects on Na-intercalation properties. Nonetheless, most of the methods to produce oxygen vacancies require harsh reducing conditions like annealing in H<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44 ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43-45)</a> and CO <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> or a vacuum, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> which might limit its practicality. Also considering that reducing the crystalline size of TiO<sub>2</sub> could lead to higher reversible sodiation capacity, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> it would be significant and desirable to design a nanosized black anatase titania utilizing an easily feasible approach for application in high-rate SIBs and to explore the effects of oxygen vacancies on its sodium storage properties.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we investigate, for the first time, the enhanced sodium storage performances of black anatase titania, which is constructed as a nanostructure via an efficient reducing route to integrate the benefits of improved electronic conductivity and facilitated Na-ion transport. This black anatase with oxygen vacancies in both bulk structure and surface presents remarkable high rate capabilities and long cycling life when utilized as anode for SIBs. The considerably positive effects of oxygen vacancies on the properties of sodium storage in black anatase structure are deeply probed by combining with detailed experiments and density functional theory (DFT) calculations. It is believed that this study would not only provide insights into the enhanced sodium storage of oxygen-deficient black titania but also be highly informative for structure designing and the optimization of electrode materials for energy storage.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">2 Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i3" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i3">2.1 Materials Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">In a typical procedure, 6 mL of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT) and 0.4 mL of acetic acid were dissolved in 40 mL of ethanol to form a homogeneous sol, named as mixture A. Meanwhile, 2.4 g of oxalic acid (OA) was dissolved in 60 mL of water–ethanol solution (2:1 in volume), named as solution B. Then, the mixture A was added dropwise into solution B under vigorous stirring at a temperature of 55 °C. After being further stirred for 3 h, the resulting precipitate was collected by centrifuge and washed by ethanol and deionized water for several times, followed by drying for 12 h at the temperature of 60 °C in an oven. Titania precursor Ti(OH)<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (HTO) was obtained by the reaction between TBOT and OA. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare black titania, we first calcined the precursor HTO in air at 350 °C for 2 h, followed by being ground together uniformly with NaBH<sub>4</sub> (2:1 in mass) as reductant and then heated in Ar atmosphere at 350 °C for 2 h. The resulting completely black sample was washed by 0.1 mol L<sup>–1</sup> HCl and deionized water repeatedly and collected by centrifuge. After being dried at 70 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h, the black TiO<sub>2</sub> powder was obtained and named B-TO for brevity. For comparison, the nanocrystalline white TiO<sub>2</sub> was prepared via calcining HTO in air at 350 °C for 4 h and named W-TO.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.2 Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) was carried out utilizing a Bruker D8 diffractometer with monochromatic Cu Kα radiation (the wavelength of 1.5406 Å) with a scan rate of 2°/min. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM, using FEI Quanta 200) together with transmission electron microscopy (TEM, using JEM-2100F) were employed in the characterization of as-prepared samples morphology. Raman spectra were tested using Renishaw InVia with wire 4.2. Brunauere-Emmette-Teller (BET, BELSORP-Mini II) surface area was obtained from the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, using K-Alpha 1063) was performed to detect the chemical composition of the samples’ surfaces. The DC electrical conductivities were tested on disc-shaped samples by direct four-point probe conductivity measurements (SDY-40, Guangdong, China). Electron spin resonance (ESR, using JES-FA200) measurements were conducted by utilizing the micro frequency of 9057.904 MHz for the white TiO<sub>2</sub> sample and 9059.228 MHz for the black TiO<sub>2</sub> sample.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.3 Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The assembly of sodium half-cells was conducted in a MBraun glovebox full of argon atmosphere to investigate the properties of as-prepared samples in sodium storage. The powder of obtained active material (70%) was mixed with 15% super P as the conductive agent and 15% carboxymethyl cellulose as the binder in deionized water, and the formed homogeneous slurry was painted on a copper foil, which was subsequently dried for 12 h at 100 °C in a vacuum oven. The copper foil loaded by active materials were pressed and cut as the electrode slices. Metallic Na was employed as the counter electrode, and Celgard 2400 was used as the separator. The electrolyte was composed of 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate and added by 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Cyclic voltammetric (CV) were measured on Solartron Analytical to examine the electrochemical reaction using the assembled cell in the voltage range of 3–0.01 V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). Galvanostatic charge–discharge measurements were carried out on the Arbin battery cycler (BT2000) in 3–0.01 V (versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). The electrochemical impedance measurements (EIS) were performed on Solartron Analytical at an AC voltage of 5 mV amplitude in the frequency range of 100 kHz–0.01 Hz after 10 discharge–charge cycles. All electrochemical tests were conducted at room temperature.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.4 Calculation Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were carried out on the basis of generalized gradient approximation (GGA) exchange-correlation energy function utilizing ultrasoft pseudopotential (USPP) formalism, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> and the cutoff of the kinetic energy was set to 300 eV for all calculations in reciprocal space. The k-points were sampled on Monkhorst–Pack grid of 6 × 6 × 7 for the unit cell, and the maximum self-consistent field convergence tolerance was &lt;2 × 10<sup>–6</sup> eV atom<sup>–1</sup>. The bond-energy tolerance was 1 × 10<sup>–5</sup> eV for the density of states (DOS) and partial density of states (PDOS) calculations.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3 Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.1 Structure, Morphology, and Composition Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structures of the as-prepared black TiO<sub>2</sub> (B-TO) and white TiO<sub>2</sub> (W-TO) were characterized by XRD, and the observed and calculated patterns were depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a. The almost identical peak centers of B-TO and W-TO at 25.28°, 37.80°, 48.05°, 53.89°, 55.06°, and 62.69° were clearly observed, corresponding to (1 0 1), (0 0 4), (2 0 0), (1 0 5), (2 0 1), and (2 0 4) crystal faces of tetragonal anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> indexed to the JCPDS card 21–1272 with a space group of I41/amd. After Rietveld refinement, the lattice parameters of the tetragonal anatase phase were <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 3.7867 Å and <i>c</i> = 9.4938 Å for W-TO (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub>= 11.3% <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 8.29%), and <i>a</i> = <i>b</i> = 3. 7908 Å and <i>c</i> = 9.5108 Å for B-TO (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub>= 14.2% <i>R</i><sub>p</sub> = 6.73%). The slightly greater lattice parameters along the <i>a</i>- and <i>c</i>- axes of B-TO were probably associated with the partial formation of Ti<sup>3+</sup> in its lattice, considering the larger ionic radius of Ti<sup>3+</sup> than that of Ti<sup>4+</sup> (Ti<sup>3+</sup>: 0.56 Å, Ti<sup>4+</sup>: 0.48 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> It was found that after TiO<sub>2</sub> was reduced by NaBH<sub>4</sub> at 350 °C, the intensity of the diffraction peaks of B-TO was a bit weaker than those of W-TO, revealing that the crystallinity was slightly decreased during the strong reduction procedure, although the crystal phase remained mostly unchanged. Besides, the broader full width at half-maximum (fwhm) suggested that the crystallite was smaller for B-TO. Utilizing the Scherrer formula (<i>L</i> = <i>k</i>λ/βcos θ, β is the peak width of the diffraction peak profile at half-maximum height resulting from small crystallite size in radians, <i>k</i> is a constant related to crystallite shape normally used as 0.9) with the broadening of the (1 0 1) peaks, the crystal sizes along the [101] direction for W-TO and B-TO were calculated to be 22.5 and 18.7 nm, respectively. Additionally, no other peaks resulting from the presence of impurities were noticed in the B-TO spectrum, indicating that the possible borides were fully removed after acid-washing and the pure anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> obtained. Alongside the XRD patterns, the corresponding visual photographs of TiO<sub>2</sub> powders were displayed, where the B-TO showed a totally black color, and the air-annealed TiO<sub>2</sub> was purely white.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of W-TO and B-TO with respective Rietveld refinement results and their related visual photographs. (b) Raman spectra for W-TO and B-TO; the inset is the magnified view of the most intense <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> peak of the two samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Structural properties of B-TO and W-TO were further analyzed by Raman scattering, as displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b. Both samples exhibited typical bands of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, while more broadened peaks were observed for B-TO, which was in accordance with the literature about black TiO<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref50" aria-label="reference 41 and 50">(41, 50)</a> Also note that the main Raman active mode of W-TO at around 145.2 cm<sup>–1</sup> (<i>E</i><sub>g</sub>) shifted toward higher wavenumbers after the introduction of OVs (B-TO: 149.7 cm<sup>–1</sup>). It has been reported that the blue-shift and broadening of the <i>E</i><sub>g</sub> mode could be caused by the finite size of grains (&lt;10 nm) or the decreasing of correlation length resulting from localized defects like OVs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Because the crystalline sizes of the samples were no less than 18 nm, the presence of OVs should be the main reason for the blue-shift and broadening of the Raman peak.</div><div class="NLM_p">The morphologies of B-TO and W-TO were shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>. Both of the samples were composed of numbers of nanoparticles according to the SEM images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a,b). The TEM images of W-TO in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c clearly depicted that the diameters of the solid nanoparticles with smooth surfaces were around 30 nm. For comparison, in the same resolution TEM of B-TO in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d, there were no obvious changes in the size of particles between B-TO and W-TO, while the slight irregularity of the particle shape, as well as the roughness and looseness of surfaces, were found for B-TO, which might be more facilitated for sodium diffusion. According to the N<sub>2</sub> absorption and desorption isotherms in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, the specific surface area of B-TO was calculated to be 106.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>, high than that of W-TO (80.72 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), which could be ascribed to its more rough and loose surface structure. As demonstrated in the HRTEM image of a single particle in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e,f, lattice fringes of approximately 0.35 nm for both samples were indexed to the (101) lattice plane of anatase. Notably, an obvious disordered surface layer could be observed coating on the crystalline core of B-TO, which was also found on black TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles in some literature <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref44 ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref44 ref51" aria-label="reference 40, 44 and 51">(40, 44, 51)</a> and might be a reconstructed layer with oxygen vacancies consisting of a disordered Ti<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> shell and a transition region of connection according to the very recent report by Tian et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> which was produced in the NaBH<sub>4</sub> reduction process.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a) W-TO and (b) B-TO. TEM images of (c) W-TO and (d) B-TO. HRTEM images of (e) W-TO and (f) B-TO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To analyze the surface chemical composition of the B-TO and W-TO, we performed XPS as exhibited in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>. The survey scan of XPS (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) showed typical TiO<sub>2</sub> spectra, and no other peaks were seen except for Ti, O, and C, eliminating the existence of impurities and doping by alien elements, which was also verified in the EDX spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The detailed information on the chemical state of titanium ion of B-TO was clarified by the Ti 2p core level XPS spectra in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, where two major peaks at 458.7 and 464.5 eV were attributed to the typical Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p <sub>3/2</sub> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks, and two shoulder peaks at 456.9 and 462.3 eV should be ascribed to Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p <sub>3/2</sub> and Ti<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, demonstrating the containing of Ti<sup>3+</sup> at B-TO, which accorded well with the previous literature <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26 ref41 ref42 ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref41 ref42 ref53" aria-label="reference 26, 41, 42 and 53">(26, 41, 42, 53)</a> and could account for the existence of disordered layer on B-TO nanoparticles observed in the HRTEM pattern. Meanwhile, a semiquantitative analysis of the chemical state of B-TO was performed in terms of Ti 2p XPS spectra, and the Ti<sup>3+</sup> molar proportion of of ∼15% was obtained. In contrast, the Ti 2p XPS spectra of W-TO in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c only showed two characterized Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p <sub>3/2</sub> and Ti<sup>4+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks at 458.8 and 464.5 eV, respectively. In the O 1s XPS spectra for both samples (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d), the broad peak could be resolved into two peaks centered at 530.1 and 532.1 eV, attributed to Ti–O and surface −OH bondings, separately. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> By comparison, the proportion of the latter peak of B-TO was relatively higher than that of W-TO, resulting from more oxygen-defective sites that tended to bind with hydrogen atoms and formed more hydroxyl surface groups. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) The survey scan of XPS of W-TO and B-TO. (b) Ti 2p XPS spectrum of B-TO. (c) Ti 2p XPS spectrum of W-TO. (d) O 1s XPS spectrum of W-TO and B-TO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">ESR spectroscopy was carried out to detect the circumstances of unemployed electrons in the TiO<sub>2</sub> molecules of the two samples and was displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>. No ESR peak was found in W-TO spectrum, while B-TO could present two strong signals. The one at <i>g</i> = 2.001 was attributed to the oxygen vacancies in the bulk TiO<sub>2</sub> that could form the Ti<sup>3+</sup> paramagnetic center and trap the electrons, and the other signal at <i>g</i> = 1.946 was corresponding to the surface Ti<sup>3+</sup> ions, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53 ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 53, 55 and 56">(53, 55, 56)</a> which was in good agreement with the outcome of the Ti 2p XPS. In consequence, the ESR analyzation confirmed that the oxygen vacancies induced Ti<sup>3+</sup> ions were not only located on the surface but also in the lattice of the bulk of B-TO.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. ESR spectra of W-TO and B-TO.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9">3.2 Electrochemical Properties for Sodium Storage</h3><div class="NLM_p">The galvanostatic charge–discharge capabilities for B-TO and W-TO were obtained at a constant current density of 0.2 C (equal to 33.6 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) for 200 cycles and were compared as shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. Distinct differences between the two samples were clearly observed in the cycles of preliminary section. A large discharge specific capacity of 444.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was yielded by B-TO in the initial cycle, and the corresponding charge specific capacity was 178.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which were much higher than those of W-TO, whose initial discharge and charge capacities were 262.4 and 111.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. During the subsequent cycles, the capabilities of the both samples exhibited an increase trendency, while the rising range and duration notably varied. For B-TO, the charge capacity climbed up to 205.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 15th cycle with a growth rate of 13.9% and was kept steady in the following cycles, retaining 205.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 200 cycles, which was a comparable capacity value with excellent cycling retention to the reported anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> anode for SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref56 ref57 ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref56 ref57 ref58" aria-label="reference 21 and 56 to 58">(21, 56-58)</a> With regard to W-TO, it crept up by 67.9% in the 85th cycle until a charge capacity plateau of 187.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was reached, which maintained stable afterward, and the preserved capacity was 184.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the 200th cycle. That phenomenon above was termed as an electrochemical activation process of nanosized anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> anode material for sodium ion batteries; that is, the reversible sodium insertion was kinetically hindered and needed repeated charging–discharging to functionalize the inner part of anatase, which was also reported in some previous literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 23 to 25">(23-25)</a> According to Yang et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> an amount of trivalent titanium ions were detected at the fully activated anatase, which meant that sodium ions were irreversibly trapped into the anatase lattice, and the Ti<sup>3+</sup> emerged correspondingly during the activation process. Therefore, the activation process of sodium storage might be triggered by an increased conductivity derived from the irreversibly generated Ti<sup>3+</sup>, which could also refer to the case of lithium. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a> That might be the key reason for the enhanced sodium storage initially and lessened duration of activation, which was the notable self-activated properties for B-WO containing Ti<sup>3+</sup> in the surfaces and lattices itself. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b displayed the Coulombic efficiencies of the two samples during the 0.2 C discharge–charge cycles. The initial cycle Coulombic efficiencies were 40.3% and 42.6% for B-TO and W-TO, respectively, which were typical values of natase TiO<sub>2</sub> together with its composite materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref30 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref30 ref59" aria-label="reference 8, 9, 30 and 59">(8, 9, 30, 59)</a> Such a low Coulombic efficiency was primarily caused by the irreversible sodiation initially along with partial decomposition of TiO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> and the formation of SEI film as well as some side reactions with the electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The efficiency of B-TO rose significantly to 95.1% in the sixth cycle and then reached up to 99.5% in the 30th cycle and sustained over that value throughout the following cycles. As to W-TO, it took 10 loops to ascend to 95.2% and 70 cycles to 99.1%, and kept around that value subsequently. In comparison, B-TO required fewer loops to elevate the efficiency and exhibited a higher value during further cycling, revealing a superior reversibility in sodium storage.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Galvanostatic cycling capabilities and (b) Coulombic efficiency of B-TO and W-TO at the rate of 0.2 C. (c) Galvanostatic discharge and charge voltage profiles for B-TO and (d) W-TO of different cycles at the rate of 0.2 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The voltage profiles of B-TO and W-TO in different cycles at 0.2 C were displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c,d, which present the typical electrochemical characteristics of anatase. The first discharge curve could be divided into three regions (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>) referring to the previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref24" aria-label="reference 8 and 24">(8, 24)</a> In region A, the voltage dropped rapidly from the open-circuit voltage to 1.5 V (B-TO) or 1.0 V (W-TO), mainly relevant to a pseudocapacitive sodium-ion adsorption. A slower trend of decrease in voltage was observed when further discharged to 0.3 V (Region B), where the main uptake of amounts of sodium ions happened. As suggested by Wu et al., <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> partial and irreversible decomposition of TiO<sub>2</sub> and reduction of Ti<sup>4+</sup> to metallic titanium to form a sodium titanate phase happened in the initial sodiation of anatase, which will no longer participate in the electrochemical redox. During the following discharge–charge process, the reversible (de)insertion into and out of the anatase host structure occurred mostly in the voltage range of 0.8 to 0.01 V, based on the Ti<sup>4+/3+</sup> redox couple. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref33" aria-label="reference 8 and 33">(8, 33)</a> In the 20th cycle profile, the voltage plateau around 0.8 V was better-defined of B-TO than that in the third cycle, while it was still ambiguous in W-TO, which was ascribed to different activation behavior of the two samples as has analyzed above. Moreover, the curves of the 20th, 100th, and 200th profiles of B-TO almost overlapped, indicating stable electrochemical performances during the continuous charge–discharge process.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical behavior of the samples as anode for sodium-ion batteries was investigated by cyclic voltammograms (CVs) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and the initial three cycles profiles of B-TO and W-TO were depicted in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a> a,b, respectively. In the first cathodic sweep, the broad peak at approximately 1.2 V (Peak C) was associated with the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and the decomposition of electrolyte impurities and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) additive as well as other organic material, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref60" aria-label="reference 29 and 60">(29, 60)</a> which was typically irreversible. Besides, the causes of the rather broad peak at around 0.5 V were still not completely clarified, yet considering the sodiation peaks emerged in the following two cyclic scans at about 0.69 V (B-TO) or 0.65 V (W-TO), marked as Peak A, that peak did not vanish but shifted to the higher potentials, probably resulted from a decreased overpotential for the reversible sodium-ion storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> For the anodic sweeps, both B-TO and W-TO exhibited a oxidative peak at around 0.83 V (Peak B) referring to the typical desodiation of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> which was successively pronounced during the subsequent cycles, and manifesting there was an activation process in sodiation–desodiation, in agreement with the initial increase in the capacities in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. Moreover, it should be noted that the peak current intensity of B-TO was relatively larger than that of W-TO, indicating a preferable sodium storage behavior for B-TO in the initial numbers of cycles. For the sake of investigating electrochemical process after cycling, CV tests with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were performed after 200 cycles at 0.2 C as presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c, where the couple of redox peaks were better-shaped and became stronger compared with the original curves, which should be assigned to the electrochemical redox of Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> and also testified that the sodiation was enhanced after accomplished activation. By comparison, the peaks of B-TO were stronger and better shaped not only in the initial CV curves (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig6" aria-label="Figure 6">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b) but also after cycling, demonstrating that a more favorable sodium insertion could be enabled into the host structure of B-TO. Notably, higher sodiation voltage as well as lower desodiation voltage were observed for B-TO, and accordingly, the deviation between the anodic and cathodic peak of B-TO (Δ<i>V</i> = 0.14 V) was smaller than that of W-TO (Δ<i>V</i> = 0.27 V), suggesting a more feasible Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction was likely due to the improved kinetics <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> and a better reversibility of B-TO in cycling, which could also be confirmed by the Coulombic efficiency records in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Cyclic voltammograms of initial three cycles tested from fresh Na-ion batteries of (a) B-TO and (b) W-TO at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; (c) compared cyclic voltammograms of B-TO and W-TO at the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> tested after 200 discharge–charge cycles at 0.2 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further evaluate the long cycling stability at a higher rate, we discharged and charged those two samples at a constant rate of 1 C over 500 cycles, as presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a. The activation proceeded more slowly compared with the cycling at 0.2 C, and likewise, W-TO took more cycles to be completely activated than B-TO. For W-TO, the charge capacity climbed from 104.4 to 145.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in the initial 150 cycles, and a capacity of 134.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was kept with the retention of 92.4% after discharge–charge for 500 cycles at 1 C (compared with the fully activated status). In regard to B-TO, it increased from 167.6 to 186.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in 70 cycles, and after 500 cycles, it still maintained 185.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, holding the retention of as high as 99.1%, which illuminated an extraordinary enduring stability. Additionally, the Coulombic efficiency kept around 99.7% during cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>), which was an attractive value for practical utilization in full Na cells and further verified the excellent sustainability of B-TO.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) Cycling performances of B-TO and W-TO at the rate of 1 C. (b) Rate performances of B-TO and W-TO at elevated rates showed by Na<sup>+</sup> extraction capacity vs cycle number. Corresponding voltage profiles at various rates of (c) B-TO and (d) W-TO. (e) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy of B-TO and W-TO after 10 cycles at the rate of 0.2 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The rate capabilities of the two samples were tested at escalated current densities and were exhibited in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b–d. To have a more precise appraisal, we discharged and charged the electrodes at a very low current density 0.05 C in the first cycle to be fully activated initially. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref30" aria-label="reference 24 and 30">(24, 30)</a> Regarding W-TO, the charge specific capacity was measured to be 189.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 °C and declined rapidly to 170.8, 147.3, 115.3, 72.9, and 38.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. As for B-TO, the charge specific capacities of 207.6, 199.1, 188.9, 170.5, and 140.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> were delivered at the rates of 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, and 5 °C, separately. Even when the current densities increased to high rates of 10, 15, and 20 C, it was still able to yield capacities of 115.0, 101.8, and 91.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, and as the current density went back to 0.5 °C, the specific capacity recovered to 199.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, illustrating a considerably admirable high-rate performance of B-TO, which was much superior to W-TO as well as other pristine anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> anode materials in previous reports, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref21" aria-label="reference 8, 9 and 21">(8, 9, 21)</a> and could hopefully enable it to be practically utilized in high-power sodium-ion batteries. The greatly enhanced high-rate performances of B-TO indicated the crucial benefits of OVs, which could significantly improve the electron-transfer process. Indeed, direct measurements revealed a much higher electronic conductivity of B-TO (4.3 × 10<sup>–4</sup>S cm<sup>–1</sup>) than pristine W-TO (2.1 × 10<sup>–8</sup>S cm<sup>–1</sup>). Furthermore, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted on the corresponding sodium batteries of the two samples, which were tested after 10 cycles at 0.2 C and charged to the voltage of 0.8 V. The Nyquist plots were presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig7" aria-label="Figure 7">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>e. Generally, the diameter of the semicircle in the intermediate frequency region signified the impedance of the charge transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref61" aria-label="reference 61">(61)</a> Obviously, the charge-transfer resistance of B-TO was much lower than that of W-TO, which confirmed a more favorable charge-transfer process on the electrode–electrolyte resulting from the introduction of oxygen vacancies.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10">3.3 Understanding the Role of Oxygen Vacancies</h3><div class="NLM_p">To acquire fundamental insights into the role of oxygen vacancies to the electrical structures and sodiation properties of anatase, we performed DFT calculations on anatase, anatase with oxygen vacancies (anatase-OV), sodiated anatase, and sodiated anatase-OV. The obtained DOS patterns and PDOS patterns of Ti, O, Na for each structure were displayed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>i (a–d) in the range of −25–12 eV, which exhibited the discrete valence band (VB) and conduction band (CB). It can be seen from the DOS of anatase-OV that after introducing OV into anatase lattice, the Femi level shifted to the CB, meaning electrons were the main carriers in this structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref62" aria-label="reference 62">(62)</a> Besides, the Ti 3d orbits became more delocalized, leading to an increase in the CB width and a narrowing of the band gap between VB and CB, which implied the electron conductivity was improved, which was well in accord with the measured conductivity of B-TO and W-TO as well as the outcomes of the previous reports. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 39 and 40">(39, 40)</a> After Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the lattice of anatase and anatase-OV, the CB and VB both moved negatively, while a smaller extent of movement was observed for sodiated anatase-OV. Moreover, the electron orbits of Ti, O, and Na of sodiated anatase-OV overlapped in more parts compared with those of pristine anatase, manifesting in a higher activity toward Na ions for anatase-OV.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/medium/am-2016-011835_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0009.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (i) Calculated density of states (DOS) and partial density states (PDOS, marked by the line of red for O, green for Ti, and dark red for Na) of (a) anatase, (b) anatase-Ov, (c) Na-inserted anatase, and (d) Na-inserted anatase-OV and (ii) their corresponding structure models.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/large/am-2016-011835_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.6b01183&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Na ion insertion process was also investigated with DFT calculations to obtain the energy barriers of sodiation, termed by the energy change (Δ<i>E</i><sub>sodiation</sub>) before and after Na ions insertion using the following formula: <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eqi"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/asset/images/am-2016-011835_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(i)</span></span>where <i>E</i><sub>sodiated&nbsp;TiO<sub>2</sub></sub>, <i>E</i><sub>TiO<sub>2</sub></sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> represented the total energy of sodiated TiO<sub>2</sub>, bulk TiO<sub>2</sub>, and Na ions, respectively. The final structure models from panels a–c for anatase, anatase-OV, sodiated anatase, and sodiated anatase-OV were shown alongside their corresponding DOS patterns in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig8" aria-label="Figure 8">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>ii (a–d), and those in the ball–stick styles were depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S5 and S6</a>, attached by the Mulliken atomic populations in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–4</a>. The obtained <i>E</i><sub>sodiated&nbsp;TiO<sub>2</sub></sub>, <i>E</i><sub>TiO<sub>2</sub></sub>, and <i>E</i><sub>Na</sub> for each model was presented in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a>, from which the average energy barriers Δ<i>E</i><sub>sodiation</sub> for two Na ions inserted into unit cells of pristine anatase and anatase-OV were calculated to be 5.63 and 4.75 eV, respectively. Therefore, the lower sodiated energy barriers suggested it was more energetically favorable for Na insertion into anatase-OV than the pristine anatase structure.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Integrating the calculations analyze with the experimental results, the superiorities of electrochemical performances of black anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> as anode for sodium ion batteries stimulated by oxygen vacancies was mainly ascribed to the following positive factors. On the one hand, the intrinsic conductivity of anatase was improved derived from the narrowed band gap of oxygen-deficient anatase, contributing to the significantly accelerated electron transfer and boosted kinetics of Na ions uptake and release. On the other hand, the energy barrier of sodiation was lowered, and in the meantime, the activity of crystal structure toward Na ions was enhanced after the introduction of oxygen vacancies, giving rise to a more favorable Na ions insertion into anatase structure. These merits synergistically functioned for the facilitated sodium accommodation, made the activation process in the charge–discharge process faster, and remarkably promoted high-rate capabilities.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">4 Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The black anatase titania was first utilized as the anode in sodium-ion batteries, which was efficiently prepared as nanostructure of ∼30 nm in diameter and verified to contain oxygen vacancies in both bulk structure and the surfaces. It yielded a decent reversible specific capacity of 207.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the rate of 0.2 C and still delivered 91.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the high rate of 20 C. After the charge–discharge process of over 500 cycles at 1 C, the capacity of 185.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> was maintained with a retention of as high as 99.1%, indicating a long cycling stability. Compared with the pristine white anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>, black anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> presented higher initial capacities, shorter electrochemical activation duration in cycling, stronger and closer redox peaks in cyclic voltammograms, better reversibility, and far more excellent high-rate capabilities, all demonstrating the vitally prominent role of oxygen vacancies to its sodium storage properties, which can not only significantly enhance the intrinsic electrical conductivity of anatase but also improve the kinetics of the Na uptake–release process. Furthermore, as illuminated by DFT calculations, the sodiation energy barrier was lower for anatase with oxygen vacancies than that of the pristine one, suggesting the more favorable Na intercalation could be enabled, according well with its superior electrochemical performances. Bestowed by the integrated merits of oxygen vacancies and the shortened Na ion diffusion length owing to nanostructure, this nanosized black anatase could serve as a promising anode candidate for SIBs with admirable durability and remarkable high-rate performances.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b01183" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b01183</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Figure showing the nitrogen absorption and desorption isotherms, EDX patterns, an initial discharge curves comparison, efficiencies in cycling at 1 C, and structure models of calculation in ball–stick styles. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.6b01183/suppl_file/am6b01183_si_001.pdf">am6b01183_si_001.pdf (910.97 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b01183" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Slater, Michael D.; Kim, Donghan; Lee, Eungje; Doeff, Marca; Johnson, Christopher S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljUDqgInkcGTA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/nl303305c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Microstructural Evolution of Tin Nanoparticles during In Situ Sodium Insertion and Extraction</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Jiang Wei; Liu, Xiao Hua; Mao, Scott X.; Huang, Jian Yu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The microstructural changes and phase transformations of tin nanoparticles during electrochem. sodiation were studied with a nanosized sodium ion battery using in situ TEM.  The first sodiation process occurred in two steps; i.e., the cryst. Sn nanoparticles were initially sodiated via a two-phase mechanism with a migrating phase boundary to form a Na-poor, amorphous NaxSn alloy (x ∼ 0.5), which was further sodiated to several Na-rich amorphous phases and finally to the crystd. Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75) via a single-phase mechanism.  The volumetric expansion was ∼60% in the first step and 420% after the second step.  However, despite the huge expansion, cracking or fracture was not obsd., which is attributed to the second step of the single-phase sodiation that accommodates large portion of the sodiation-induced stress over the entire particle.  Excellent cyclability was also obsd. during the reversible sodiation/desodiation cycles, showing great potential of Sn nanoparticles as a robust electrode material for rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq4-_RnLjqf7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lggHxQ6iRr3QQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFGqs7rO&amp;md5=3a9b283edadab9ac6dda7ef5c8bd18b7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06476C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sb porous hollow microspheres as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sb porous hollow microspheres as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hou, Hongshuai; Jing, Mingjun; Yang, Yingchang; Zhang, Yan; Zhu, Yirong; Song, Weixin; Yang, Xuming; Ji, Xiaobo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb porous hollow microspheres (PHMSs) were prepd. by a replacement reaction employing Zn microspheres (MSs) as templates.  The obtained Sb PHMSs were first applied as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and showed a high reversible capacity of 617 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 100 cycles, exhibiting a high capacity retention of 97.2%.  Even at a high c.d. of 3200 mA g-1, the reversible capacity can also reach 312.9 mA h g-1.  The superior electrochem. performance of Sb PHMSs can be attributed to the unique structural characteristic of Sb with porous and hollow structure, which can accommodate the vol. change and facilitate the Na+ diffusion during the sodiation and desodiation process.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqxRl0XZk6mYLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lggHxQ6iRr3QQ"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Red Phosphorus-Single-Walled Carbon Nanotube Composite as a Superior Anode for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yujie; Wen, Yang; Fan, Xiulin; Gao, Tao; Han, Fudong; Luo, Chao; Liou, Sz-Chian; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3254-3264</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a top alternative to Li ion batteries due to the earth abundance and low cost of Na compared with Li.  Among all proposed anode materials for SIBs, red P is a very promising candidate because it has the highest theor. capacity (∼2600 mA-h g-1).  A red P-single-walled C nanotube (denoted as red P-SWCNT) composite, in which red P is uniformly distributed between tangled SWCNTs bundles, is fabricated by a modified vaporization-condensation method.  Benefiting from the nondestructive prepn. process, the highly conductive and mech. strong SWCNT network is preserved, which enhances the cond. of the composite and stabilizes the solid electrolyte interphase.  As a result, the red P-SWCNT composite presents a high overall Na storage capacity (∼700 mA-h g-1composite at 50 mA/gcomposite), fast rate capability (∼300 mA-h g-1composite at 2000 mA/gcomposite), and stable long-term cycling performance with 80% capacity retention after 2000 sodiation-desodiation cycles.  The red P-SWCNT composite fabricated by the vaporization-condensation method significantly extends the cycling stability of P/C composite from current ∼100 cycles to ∼2000 cycles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqd3GxgIxmfmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lggHxQ6iRr3QQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXjvVOhs7c%253D&amp;md5=6ba87307633bf69051e0eb4e034c85a8</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201308354"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van Aken, P.
