                10.1021/am501772a</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D11295-11301%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26author%3DS.%2BM.%2BOh%26author%3DJ.%2BY.%2BHwang%26author%3DC.%2BS.%2BYoon%26author%3DJ.%2BLu%26author%3DK.%2BAmine%26author%3DI.%2BBelharouak%26author%3DY.%2BK.%2BSun%26title%3DHigh%2Belectrochemical%2Bperformances%2Bof%2Bmicrosphere%2BC-TiO2%2Banode%2Bfor%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbattery%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fam501772a&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b01183&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fam501772a&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit60R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">60</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High electrochemical performances of microsphere C-TiO2 anode for sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Oh, Seung-Min; Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Yoon, C. S.; Lu, Jun; Amine, Khalil; Belharouak, Illias; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">11295-11301</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-power, long-life carbon-coated TiO2 microsphere electrodes were synthesized by a hydrothermal method for sodium ion batteries, and the electrochem. properties were evaluated as a function of carbon content.  The carbon coating, introduced by sucrose addn., had an effect of suppressing the growth of the TiO2 primary crystallites during calcination.  The carbon coated TiO2 (sucrose 20 wt % coated) electrode exhibited excellent cycle retention during 50 cycles (100%) and superior rate capability up to a 30 C rate at room temp.  This cell delivered a high discharge capacity of 155 mAh gcomposite-1 at 0.1 C, 149 mAh gcomposite-1 at 1 C, and 82.7 mAh gcomposite-1 at a 10 C rate, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxoiAXO-ILG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljvEqWQYIeiuw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtVahtL7L&amp;md5=545a20e4e86c59fdac64158a8ccb76e0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref61'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit61" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA05611F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Banks, C. E.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon dots supported upon N-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> nanorods applied into sodium and lithium ion batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Mater. Chem. A</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">3</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">5648</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">5655</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C4TA05611F</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D3%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D5648-5655%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DY.%2BYang%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26author%3DM.%2BJing%26author%3DH.%2BHou%26author%3DY.%2BZhu%26author%3DL.%2BFang%26author%3DX.%2BYang%26author%3DQ.%2BChen%26author%3DC.%2BE.%2BBanks%26title%3DCarbon%2Bdots%2Bsupported%2Bupon%2BN-doped%2BTiO2%2Bnanorods%2Bapplied%2Binto%2Bsodium%2Band%2Blithium%2Bion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252FC4TA05611F&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b01183&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2FC4TA05611F&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon dots supported upon N-doped TiO2 nanorods applied into sodium and lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Yingchang; Ji, Xiaobo; Jing, Mingjun; Hou, Hongshuai; Zhu, Yirong; Fang, Laibing; Yang, Xuming; Chen, Qiyuan; Banks, Craig E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5648-5655</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">N-doped TiO2 nanorods decorated with carbon dots with enhanced elec. cond. and faster charge transfer have been fabricated utilizing a simple hydrothermal reaction process involving TiO2 powders (P25) and NaOH in the presence of carbon dots followed by ion exchange and calcination treatments.  Due to the merits of the carbon dots, doping and nanostructures, the as-designed N-doped TiO2/C dots composite utilized as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries can sustain a capacity of 185 mA-h/g with 91.6% retention even at a high rate of 10C over 1000 cycles.  It is interesting to note that the ratios of capacitive charge capacity during such high rates for the N-doped TiO2/C dots composite electrodes are higher than those at low rates, which likely explains the obsd. excellent rate capabilities.  In contrast to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries have gained more interest in energy storage grids because of the greater abundance and lower cost of sodium-contg. precursors.  The as-obtained N-doped TiO2/C dots composites reported here and utilized as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries exhibit excellent electrochem. performances, including substantial cycling stabilities (the capacity retention ratios after 300 cycles at 5C is 93.6%) and remarkable rate capabilities (176 mA-h/g at 5C, 131 mA-h/g at 20C); such performances are the best ever reported to date over other structured TiO2 or TiO2 composite materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpdQoz8zgNzhbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liTAcZibOfk0g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXislertb8%253D&amp;md5=59ffdc173c01a8ef1a6df7ea8b62f47c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref62'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit62" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta01438c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tian, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xin, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tan, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, W.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High lithium electroactivity of boron-doped hierarchical rutile submicrosphere TiO<sub>2</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Mater. Chem. A</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">2</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">10599</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">10606</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c4ta01438c</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2014%26pages%3D10599-10606%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DH.%2BTian%26author%3DF.%2BXin%26author%3DX.%2BTan%26author%3DW.%2BHan%26title%3DHigh%2Blithium%2Belectroactivity%2Bof%2Bboron-doped%2Bhierarchical%2Brutile%2Bsubmicrosphere%2BTiO2%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc4ta01438c&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.6b01183&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc4ta01438c&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit62R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">62</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High lithium electroactivity of boron-doped hierarchical rutile submicrosphere TiO2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tian, Huajun; Xin, Fengxia; Tan, Xiaojian; Han, Weiqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">27</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10599-10606</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have reported a facile method to fabricate hierarchical boron-doped rutile submicrosphere TiO2 (SMT), whose primary particles are ∼20 nm in diam.  The as-synthesized boron-doped SMT shows excellent cycling performance and rate capability in comparison with undoped TiO2 as an anode material in Lithium-Ion Batteries (LIBs).  It has a very stable capacity of ∼190 mA h g-1 for 500 cycles at 1C.  In addn., the d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. are carried out to indicate that a low concn. (&lt;1.0 at%) of boron doping could enhance the carrier mobility μ and elec. cond. σ, and thus reveal the relationship between the electronic structure of boron-doped SMT and the performances of the boron-doped SMT anode in LIBs.  Our results also clearly demonstrate the importance and advantage of the hierarchical submicrometer-sized spherical morphol. of the TiO2 anode in LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgeb17-jLe9Hw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1021/nl303305c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The microstructural changes and phase transformations of tin nanoparticles during electrochem. sodiation were studied with a nanosized sodium ion battery using in situ TEM.  The first sodiation process occurred in two steps; i.e., the cryst. Sn nanoparticles were initially sodiated via a two-phase mechanism with a migrating phase boundary to form a Na-poor, amorphous NaxSn alloy (x ∼ 0.5), which was further sodiated to several Na-rich amorphous phases and finally to the crystd. Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75) via a single-phase mechanism.  The volumetric expansion was ∼60% in the first step and 420% after the second step.  However, despite the huge expansion, cracking or fracture was not obsd., which is attributed to the second step of the single-phase sodiation that accommodates large portion of the sodiation-induced stress over the entire particle.  Excellent cyclability was also obsd. during the reversible sodiation/desodiation cycles, showing great potential of Sn nanoparticles as a robust electrode material for rechargeable batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq4-_RnLjqf7rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh5Y3X3CiSvCg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XhsFGqs7rO&amp;md5=3a9b283edadab9ac6dda7ef5c8bd18b7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA06476C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Song, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sb porous hollow microspheres as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a top alternative to Li ion batteries due to the earth abundance and low cost of Na compared with Li.  Among all proposed anode materials for SIBs, red P is a very promising candidate because it has the highest theor. capacity (∼2600 mA-h g-1).  A red P-single-walled C nanotube (denoted as red P-SWCNT) composite, in which red P is uniformly distributed between tangled SWCNTs bundles, is fabricated by a modified vaporization-condensation method.  Benefiting from the nondestructive prepn. process, the highly conductive and mech. strong SWCNT network is preserved, which enhances the cond. of the composite and stabilizes the solid electrolyte interphase.  As a result, the red P-SWCNT composite presents a high overall Na storage capacity (∼700 mA-h g-1composite at 50 mA/gcomposite), fast rate capability (∼300 mA-h g-1composite at 2000 mA/gcomposite), and stable long-term cycling performance with 80% capacity retention after 2000 sodiation-desodiation cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The prepn. and electrochem. storage behavior of MoS2 nanodots-more precisely single-layered ultrasmall nanoplates-embedded in C nanowires was studied.  The prepn. is achieved by an electrospinning process that can be easily scaled up.  The rate performance and cycling stability of both Li and Na storage are outstanding.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V.  With the help of the conductive carbon layers, the carbon-coated anatase TiO2 delivers more capacity at high rates, 104 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g), 82 mA-h/g at the 30C rate (10 A/g), and 53 mA-h/g at the 100C rate (33 A g-1).  By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).  The excellent cyclability and high-rate capability are the result of a Na+ insertion and extn. reaction into the host structure coupled with Ti4+/3+ redox reaction, as shown by x-ray absorption spectroscopy.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpadjcIpWM-W7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh5Y3X3CiSvCg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXktlGhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=4bc3a520e6f654a82cbee3b778eef0e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1039/c3cc45254a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lotfabad, E.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of the rutile-type TiO2 and Nb-doped TiO2 were investigated for the first time as Na-ion battery anodes.  Ti1-xNbxO2 thick-film electrodes without a binder and a conductive additive were prepd. using a sol-gel method followed by a gas-deposition method.  The TiO2 electrode showed reversible reactions of Na insertion/extn. accompanied by expansion/contraction of the TiO2 lattice.  Among the Ti1-xNbxO2 electrodes with x = 0-0.18, the Ti0.94Nb0.06O2 electrode exhibited the best cycling performance, with a reversible capacity of 160 mA h g-1 at the 50th cycle. The improvements in the performances are attributed to a 3 orders of magnitude higher electronic cond. of Ti0.94Nb0.06O2 compared to that of TiO2.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rutile TiO2 microspheres anchored by nanoneedle clusters, as a new class of anode materials, are successfully employed for sodium-ion batteries and manifested good energy storage behavior.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Exploring new anode materials is important for developing sodium ion batteries.  In this work, TiO2(B) nanotubes are prepd. by hydrothermal reaction in concd. NaOH soln. and subsequent calcination at 300 °C in air, and investigated for the first time as anodes for sodium ion batteries.  It is demonstrated that TiO2(B) nanotubes are several hundred nanometers in length with an outer diam. of about 10-15 nm.  Moreover, the interference fringe spacings of the nanotubes are derived from the TEM image, being about 0.56 and 0.34 nm, which are consistent with the interplanar distances of the (001) and (110) planes in the TiO2(B) phase, resp.  As anticipated, the interlayer spacing of the (001) plane in the TiO2(B) nanotubes is large enough to accommodate sodium ions.  Meanwhile, the tubular morphol. can ensure the good high rate capability and cycle stability.  Therefore, TiO2(B) nanotubes present feasible electrochem. sodium storage, offering possibilities to develop new anode materials for sodium ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr7QHLL_XKX8rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgI-Dq2g8JQNA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtFSitbjI&amp;md5=92a7ab51057c3fd3e33cf16fc50b6b8e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref16" class="rightTabRefNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit16" data-doi="10.1021/jz2012066"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiong, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Slater, M. Despite several studies of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries involving layered oxide materials, there are few low-voltage metal oxide anodes capable of operating sodium ion reversibly at room temp.  We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  We find that only amorphous large diam. nanotubes (&gt;80 nm I.D.) can support electrochem. cycling with sodium ions.  These electrodes maximize their capacity in operando and reach reversible capacity of 150 mA-h/g in 15 cycles.  We also demonstrate for the first time a full cell all-oxide sodium-ion battery using amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube anode coupled to a Na1.0Li0.2Ni0.25Mn0.75Oδ cathode at room temp. exhibiting good rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr8q2bDG7YJCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgI-Dq2g8JQNA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1ChtLbP&amp;md5=66a2a71991a01d6823c9c01024f65c74</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref17" class="rightTabRefNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b07508"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Prutsch, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wilkening, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hanzu, I.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Long-Cycle-Life Na-Ion Anodes Based on Amorphous Titania Nanotubes-Interfaces and Diffusion</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Amorphous TiO2@C nanospheres were synthesized via a template approach. The electrochem. properties of the amorphous TiO2 nanospheres and the TiO2 hollow nanospheres with different phases were characterized as anodes for the Na-ion batteries.  It was found that all the samples demonstrated excellent cyclability, which was sustainable for hundreds of cycles with little capacity fading, although the anatase TiO2 presented a capability that was better than that of the mixed anatase/rutile TiO2 or the amorphous TiO2@C.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Titanium dioxide (TiO2) has been proposed as a potential electrode material for lithium, sodium, and magnesium ion batteries.  Among the phases of TiO2, anatase, rutile, and (B)-TiO2 are the most commonly used phases for electrochem. storage, while the amorphous phase has also been shown to be a promising candidate.  We present a comparative d. functional theory study of the insertion energetics of Li, Na, and Mg into anatase, rutile, and (B)-TiO2, as well as into the amorphous phase.  Our results show that among the cryst. phases, (B)-TiO2 provides the strongest binding between TiO2 and the inserted Li/Na/Mg atom.  We also find that for all Li, Na, and Mg, the amorphous phase provides insertion sites well-dispersed in energies, with a lowest energy site more thermodynamically favorable than insertion sites in the cryst. phases.  We also obtain the localized Ti3+ states together with the formation of the defect states in the band gap, which are induced by the insertion, at the GGA level of theory (without the Hubbard correction).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpdTmOGXc5koLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljHwDWF-k_yHw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitFOntbrN&amp;md5=abed3e72191c2e118aa322f5b1969378</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref23"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref23" class="rightTabRefNumLink">23</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit23" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.11.083"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Buchholz, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bresser, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gomes Chagas, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Passerini, S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles for high power sodium-ion anodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered to be a promising low-cost alternative to common lithium-ion batteries.  Herein, we present a detailed electrochem. characterization of anatase TiO2 nanoparticles as anode material for Na-ion batteries, highlighting the substantial influence of the electrolyte compn. (salt and solvent) on the obtainable specific capacity, cycling stability, and particularly the coulombic efficiency.  TiO2-based electrodes cycled using a 1M soln. of NaClO4 in a mixt. of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate present excellent high rate capability, offering about 100 and 86 mAh g-1 at 5.5 and 11 C, resp., which is - to the best of our knowledge - the best high rate capability for all titanium-based sodium-ion anode materials reported so far.  Setting the C rate to 5.5C (i.e., 1.85 A g-1), such electrodes can be cycled for more than 1000 cycles without significant capacity decay, confirming their outstanding durability at such high specific current.  These results, in combination with its environmental friendliness and cost efficiency, render anatase TiO2 nanoparticles a promising anode material for high power sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo0_SL7CQRACLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljHwDWF-k_yHw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXpsF2m&amp;md5=f2daf85a7868d446e5a0ff57ad754ecf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201401142"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bresser, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Buchholz, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Giffin, G.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the aim of advancing anatase TiO2 anodes for Na ion batteries, cryst. TiO2 nanocubes were employed and they delivered a gradually increasing capacity during the initial cycles, termed as an activation process.  The no. of necessary discharge-charge loops for total activation is dependent on the galvanostatic c.d. (∼20 cycles at 0.2 C, or 90 cycles at 1 C).  A percentage of Ti3+ was detected after the activation, indicating an amt. of irreversibly trapped Na ions in the lattice.  After the activation process, an excellent rate capability and outstanding cycling stability were presented.  The reversible capacity reached 174, 132, and 108 mA h g-1 at rates of 1 C, 5 C, and 10 C, resp.  The capacity was sustained with a loss of &lt;10% after 1000 discharge-charge cycles at a rate of 2 C or 10 C.  The superior battery performance achieved by the nanocubes is related to the encircled {100} facets that are more favorable for Na ion attachment compared to the {001} and {101} facets, as supported by 1st-principles calcns.  From this work the authors can see the feasibility of optimizing electrode materials via rational surface structure construction based on theor. calcns.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpebO59ke4jqbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirWTHSKk8gIQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXkvFGjsrw%253D&amp;md5=a5ab3c8c5cb9855d41e67b06bcde30de</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA02873B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lian, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tian, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ionothermal synthesis of black Ti<sup>3+</sup>-doped single-crystal TiO<sub>2</sub> as an active photocatalyst for pollutant degradation and H<sub>2</sub> generation</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.                         rightTab-cas_cit28R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">28</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Iso-Oriented Anatase TiO2 Mesocages as a High Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hong Zhensheng; Zhou Kaiqiang; Huang Zhigao; Wei Mingdeng; Wei Mingdeng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Scientific reports</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">A major obstacle in realizing Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is the absence of suitable anode materials.  Herein, we firstly report the anatase TiO2 mesocages constructed by crystallographically oriented nanoparticle subunits as a high performance anode for NIBs.  The mesocages with tunable microstructures, high surface area (204 m(2) g(-1)) and uniform mesoporous structure were firstly prepared by a general synthesis method under the assist of sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS).  It's notable that the TiO2 mesocages exhibit a large reversible capacity and good rate capability.  A stable capacity of 93 mAhg(-1) can be retained after 500 cycles at 10 C in the range of 0.01-2.5 V, indicating high rate performance and good cycling stability.  This could be due to the uniform architecture of iso-oriented mesocage structure with few grain boundaries and nanoporous nature, allowing fast electron and ion transport, and providing more active sites as well as freedom for volume change during Na-ion insertion.  CV measurements demonstrate that the sodium-ion storage process of anatase mesocages is mainly controlled by pseudocapacitive behavior, which is different from the lithium-ion storage and further facilitates the high rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcQNy5_7QVOqG5Pq5LRczm8ffW6udTcc2ea89_yVUqe2erntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC28%252FgvFWrtQ%253D%253D&amp;md5=0121d7bef5cb80893b9c3204ed716cc4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.5b00633"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhou, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wen, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mi, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fu, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lei, Y.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Highly Ordered Three-Dimensional Ni-TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanoarrays as Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Ordered Three-Dimensional Ni-TiO2 Nanoarrays as Sodium Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Yang; Zhou, Min; Wen, Liaoyong; Wang, Chengliang; Zhao, Huaping; Mi, Yan; Liang, Liying; Fu, Qun; Wu, Minghong; Lei, Yong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries (SIBs) represent an effective energy storage technol. with potentially lower material costs than Li ion batteries.  Here, the electrochem. performance of SIBs, esp. rate capability, is intimately connected to the electrode design at the nanoscale by taking anatase TiO2 as an example.  Highly ordered 3-dimensional (3D) Ni-TiO2 core-shell nanoarrays were fabricated using nanoimprited AAO templating technique and directly used as anode.  The nanoarrays delivered a reversible capacity of ∼200 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at the c.d. of 50 mA h g-1 and were able to retain a capacity of ∼95 mA h g-1 at the c.d. ≤5 A g-1 and fully recover low rate capacity.  High ion accessibility, fast electron transport, and excellent electrode integrity were shown as great merits to obtain the presented electrochem. performance.  The work demonstrates the possibility of highly ordered 3-dimensional heterostructured nanoarrays as a promising electrode design for Na energy storage to alleviate the reliance on the materials' intrinsic nature and provides a versatile and cost-effective technique for the fabrication of such perfectly ordered nanostructures.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHslR0Alvw9bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lirWTHSKk8gIQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXpsFOjuro%253D&amp;md5=2843c505872489891b1e7f89f12fa7a3</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201501489"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tahir, M.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The synthesis of in situ polymer-functionalized anatase TiO2 particles using an anchoring block copolymer with hydroxamate as coordinating species is reported, which yields nanoparticles (≈11 nm) in multigram scale.  Thermal annealing converts the polymer brushes into a uniform and homogeneous carbon coating as proven by high resolution transmission electron microscopy and Raman spectroscopy.  The strong impact of particle size as well as carbon coating on the electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 is demonstrated.  Downsizing the particles leads to higher reversible uptake/release of sodium cations per formula unit TiO2 (e.g., 0.72 eq.  Na(+) (11 nm) vs only 0.56 eq.  Na(+) (40 nm)) while the carbon coating improves rate performance.  The combination of small particle size and homogeneous carbon coating allows for the excellent electrochemical performance of anatase TiO2 at high (134 mAh g(-1) at 10 C (3.35 A g(-1))) and low (≈227 mAh g(-1) at 0.1 C) current rates, high cycling stability (full capacity retention between 2nd and 300th cycle at 1 C) and improved coulombic efficiency (≈99.8%).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=LOZGTtr2ZcRca10rw1_Tc0Gy1nwu7K2mfW6udTcc2ea89_yVUqe2erntkvexoksv"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A280%3ADC%252BC1c%252Fht1Wltw%253D%253D&amp;md5=29c3c300fc394f1c426270e5e28f600e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref31" class="rightTabRefNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit31" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.5b02724"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jiao, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yuan, H.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultrasmall TiO<sub>2</sub> Nanoparticles in Situ Growth on Graphene Hybrid as Superior Anode Material for Sodium/Lithium Ion Batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">To inhibit the aggregation of TiO2 nanoparticles and to improve the electrochem. kinetics of TiO2 electrode, a hybrid material of ultra-small TiO2 nanoparticles in situ grown on rGO nanosheets was obtained by ultrasonic and reflux methods.  The size of the TiO2 particles was controlled ∼10 nm, and these particles were evenly distributed across the rGO nanosheets.  When used for the anode of a Na ion battery, the electrochem. performance of this hybrid TiO2@rGO was much improved.  A capacity of 186.6 mA h g-1 was obtained after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1, and 112.2 mA h g-1 could be maintained at 1.0 A g-1, showing a high capacity and good rate capability.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are emerging as a highly promising technol. for large-scale energy storage applications.  However, it remains a significant challenge to develop an anode with superior long-term cycling stability and high-rate capability.  Here we demonstrate that the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitance in TiO2/graphene nanocomposites enables high-rate capability and long cycle life in a sodium-ion battery.  This hybrid electrode exhibits a specific capacity of above 90 mA h g-1 at 12,000 mA g-1 (∼36 C).  The capacity is highly reversible for more than 4,000 cycles, the longest demonstrated cyclability to date.  First-principle calcns. demonstrate that the intimate integration of graphene with TiO2 reduces the diffusion energy barrier, thus enhancing the Na+ intercalation pseudocapacitive process.  The Na-ion intercalation pseudocapacitance enabled by tailor-deigned nanostructures represents a promising strategy for developing electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrGrV7K8Tq5mLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhgQxXGulD6Ig"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtF2lurzK&amp;md5=99303fd99ef59044874690750282067a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref33" class="rightTabRefNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2015.06.017"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hwang, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Myung, S.-T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, J.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Abouimrane, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Belharouak, I.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Y.-K.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ultrafast sodium storage in anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles embedded on carbon nanotubes</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Energy</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">16</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">218</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">226</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The main disadvantage of using transition metal oxides for Na+-ion batteries is the sluggish kinetics of insertion of Na+ ions into the structure.  Here, we introduce nanosized anatase TiO2 that is partially doped with fluorine (TiO2-δFδ) to form electro-conducting trivalent Ti3+ as an ultrafast Na+ insertion material for use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  In addn., the F-doped TiO2-δFδ is modified by electro-conducting carbon nanotubes (CNTs) to further enhance the elec. cond.  The composite F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is produced in a one-pot hydrothermal reaction.  X-ray diffraction and microscopic studies revealed that nanocryst. anatase-type TiO2-δFδ particles, in which fluorine is present with TiO2 particles, are loaded on the CNTs.  This yields a high elec. cond. of approx. 5.8 S cm-1.  The first discharge capacity of the F-doped TiO2 embedded in CNTs is approx. 250 mA h (g-oxide)-1, and is retained at 97% after 100 cycles.  As expected, a high-rate performance was achieved even at the 100 C discharging rate (25 A g-1) where the composite material demonstrated a capacity of 118 mA h g-1 under the 0.1 C-rate charge condition.  The present work also highlights a significant improvement in the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions when the material was charged and discharged under the same rate of 35 C (8.75 A g-1), delivering approx. 90 mA h (g-oxide)-1.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplvYUQuk3uRLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhgQxXGulD6Ig"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtFeit77J&amp;md5=9db54ffbf3545c536663ec3bad748b95</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref34" class="rightTabRefNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit34" data-doi="10.1039/C4CC10020D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yan, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Bai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, T.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sun, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Pan, L.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sn-doped TiO<sub>2</sub> nanotubes as superior anode materials for sodium ion batteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are considered to be a promising low-cost alternative to common lithium-ion batteries in the areas where specific energy is less crit.  Among all the anode materials studied so far, TiO2 is very promising due to its low operating voltage, high capacity, nontoxicity, and low prodn. cost.  Herein, we present Nb-doped anatase TiO2 nanoparticles with high capacity, excellent cycling performance, and excellent rate capability.  The optimized Nb-doped TiO2 anode delivers high reversible capacities of 177 mA h g-1 at 0.1C and 108.8 mA h g-1 at 5C, in contrast to 150.4 mA h g-1 at 0.1C and only 54.6 mA h g-1 at 5C for the pristine TiO2. The specific capacity of Zn doped mesoporous TiO2 microspheres was significantly higher than that of TiO2 nanopowder at high charge-discharge rates.                         rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Understanding the fast lithium storage performance of hydrogenated TiO2 nanoparticles</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Yong; Hao, Bo; Wang, Dong; Chen, Ge; Markweg, Eric; Albrecht, Arne; Schaaf, Peter</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">46</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Anatase TiO2 is considered as one of the promising anode materials for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) because of its nontoxicity, safety, and excellent capacity retention.  However, the poor rate capability of TiO2 electrodes, caused by the low elec. cond. and lithium diffusion coeff., strongly hinders its practical application in high power LIBs.  Herein, we report on the fast lithium storage performance of hydrogenated anatase TiO2 nanoparticles (H-TiO2) prepd. by a H2 plasma treatment.  The scan-rate dependence of the cyclic voltammetry (CV) anal. reveals that the improved rate capability of H-TiO2 results from the enhanced contribution of pseudocapacitive lithium storage on the particle surface.  Combined with the structural properties of H-TiO2, it is suggested that the disordered surface layers and Ti3+ species of H-TiO2 play an important role in the improvement of pseudocapacitive lithium storage.  The results help to understand the fast lithium storage performance of H-TiO2 and might pave the way for further studies of other hydrogenated metal oxide electrodes for high power LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoChzNuJ-ufmbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgVe9v1JwmTFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhslers7%252FP&amp;md5=82e3d4e3691b3f4bc5b3d19183bbcc7e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA01718A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Eom, J.-Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lim, S.-J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lee, S.-M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ryu, W.-H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kwon, H.-S.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Black titanium oxide nanoarray electrodes for high rate Li-ion microbatteries</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Anatase titania nanotube arrays were fabricated by means of anodization of Ti foil and annealed at 400° in resp. CO and N2 gases for 3 h.  Electrochem. impedance spectroscopy study showed that CO annealed arrays possessed a noticeably lower charge-transfer resistance as compared with arrays annealed in N2 gas under otherwise the same conditions.  TiO2 nanotube arrays annealed in CO possessed much improved lithium ion intercalation capacity and rate capability than N2 annealed samples.  At a high charge/discharge c.d. of 320 mA/g, the initial discharge capacity in CO annealed arrays was found to be as high as 223 mA-h/g, 30% higher than in N2 annealed arrays, ∼164 mA-h/g.  After 50 charge/discharge cycles, the discharge capacity in CO annealed arrays remained at ∼179 mA-h/g.  The improved intercalation capacity and rate capability could be attributed to the presence of surface defects like Ti-C species and Ti3+ groups with oxygen vacancies, which not only improved the charge-transfer cond. of the arrays but also possibly promoted phase transition.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpE_MN7vZdz2bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lja_LtOJ8EoDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXhtFWksrvO&amp;md5=d89e6407aeeada22adbc2f1b66470959</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1039/c1cc10537j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tian, B.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">An economic method to prepare vacuum activated photocatalysts with high photo-activities and photosensitivities</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem.                         rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Scalable Synthesis of TiO2/Graphene Nanostructured Composite with High-Rate Performance for Lithium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xin, Xing; Zhou, Xufeng; Wu, Jinghua; Yao, Xiayin; Liu, Zhaoping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple and scalable method is developed to synthesize TiO2/graphene nanostructured composites as high-performance anode materials for Li-ion batteries using hydroxyl titanium oxalate (HTO) as the intermediate for TiO2.  With assistance of a surfactant, amorphous HTO can condense as a flower-like nanostructure on graphene oxide (GO) sheets.  By calcination, the HTO/GO nanocomposite can be converted to TiO2/graphene nanocomposite with well preserved flower-like nanostructure.  In the composite, TiO2 nanoparticles with an ultrasmall size of several nanometers construct the porous flower-like nanostructure which strongly attached onto conductive graphene nanosheets.  The TiO2/graphene nanocomposite is able to deliver a capacity of 230 mA h g-1 at 0.1 C (corresponding to a c.d. of 17 mA g-1), and demonstrates superior high-rate charge-discharge capability and cycling stability at charge/discharge rates up to 50 C in a half cell configuration.  Full cell measurement using the TiO2/graphene as the anode material and spinel LiMnO2 as the cathode material exhibit good high-rate performance and cycling stability, indicating that the TiO2/graphene nanocomposite has a practical application potential in advanced Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqv3MjJEb6ofbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lja_LtOJ8EoDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xhsleqt7fN&amp;md5=a82968fc83723419fde4029b48047eea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1103/PhysRevB.45.13244"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Perdew, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Utilizing solar energy for hydrogen generation and water cleaning is a great challenge due to insufficient visible-light power conversion.  Here we report a mass prodn. approach to synthesize black titania by aluminum redn.  The obtained sample possesses a unique cryst. core-amorphous shell structure (TiO2@TiO2-x).  The black titania absorbs ∼65% of the total solar energy by improving visible and IR absorption, superior to the recently reported ones (∼30%) and pristine TiO2 (∼5%).  The unique core-shell structure (TiO2@TiO2-x) and high absorption boost the photocatalytic water cleaning and water splitting.  The black titania is also an excellent photoelectrochem. electrode exhibiting a high solar-to-hydrogen efficiency (1.7%).  A large photothermic effect may enable black titania "capture" solar energy for solar thermal collectors.  The Al-reduced amorphous shell is proved to be an excellent candidate to absorb more solar light and receive more efficient photocatalysis.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqLGu41A-PKSrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lja_LtOJ8EoDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhsV2it7nI&amp;md5=1e2d4af91daa4822be74300a0a8d4935</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.5b04712"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tian, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mahjouri-Samani, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Eres, G.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sachan, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yoon, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chisholm, M.                         rightTab-cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Structure and Formation Mechanism of Black TiO2 Nanoparticles</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tian, Mengkun; Mahjouri-Samani, Masoud; Eres, Gyula; Sachan, Ritesh; Yoon, Mina; Chisholm, Matthew F.; Wang, Kai; Puretzky, Alexander A.; Rouleau, Christopher M.; Geohegan, David B.; Duscher, Gerd</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The remarkable properties of black TiO2 are due to its disordered surface shell surrounding a cryst. core.  However, the chem. compn. and the at. and electronic structure of the disordered shell and its relationship to the core remain poorly understood.  Using advanced transmission electron microscopy methods, we show that the outermost layer of black TiO2 nanoparticles consists of a disordered Ti2O3 shell.  The measurements show a transition region that connects the disordered Ti2O3 shell to the perfect rutile core consisting first of four to five monolayers of defective rutile, contg. clearly visible Ti interstitial atoms, followed by an ordered reconstruction layer of Ti interstitial atoms.  Our data suggest that this reconstructed layer presents a template on which the disordered Ti2O3 layers form by interstitial diffusion of Ti ions.  In contrast to recent reports that attribute TiO2 band-gap narrowing to the synergistic action of oxygen vacancies and surface disorder of nonspecific origin, our results point to Ti2O3, which is a narrow-band-gap semiconductor.  As a stoichiometric compd. of the lower oxidn. state Ti3+ it is expected to be a more robust at. structure than oxygen-deficient TiO2 for preserving and stabilizing Ti3+ surface species that are the key to the enhanced photocatalytic activity of black TiO2.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGp3BMIg8Q5q07Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lja_LtOJ8EoDg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhsFCjtb3F&amp;md5=8b47a66c35bb952f88e7bfa448e117b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1021/am508505n"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Li, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Gao, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Huang, B.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dai, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lu, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In-Situ-Reduced Synthesis of Ti3+ Self-Doped TiO2/g-C3N4 Heterojunctions with High Photocatalytic Performance under LED Light Irradiation</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A series of TiO2 catalysts self-doped with Ti3+ were successfully synthesized by a simple one-step solvothermal method with low-cost NaBH4 added as a reductant. The double-wall structure of the nanotube enhances the surface of TiO2 being in contact with the electrolyte soln., thus allowing an easy penetration of the alkali ions into the electrode active material.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref58"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref58" class="rightTabRefNumLink">58</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit58" data-doi="10.1021/jp9909964"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">van de Krol, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Goossens, A.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Schoonman, J.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Spatial extent of lithium intercalation in anatase TiO<sub>2</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J. Phys. Chem. B</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1999</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">103</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">7151</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">7159</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        rightTab-cas_cit58R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">58</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Spatial Extent of Lithium Intercalation in Anatase TiO2</span></div><div class="casAuthors">van de Krol, Roel; Goossens, Albert; Schoonman, Joop</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry B</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1999</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">103</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">34</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">7151-7159</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCBFK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1089-5647</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Thin smooth anatase TiO2 films were obtained by electron beam evapn. of reduced TiO2.  These films show a preferential (004) orientation when deposited on electron beam evapd. amorphous titanium.  Electrochem. lithium insertion into these films was studied with several optical and electrochem. techniques.  A coloration efficiency of 13 cm2 C-1 is found, which is twice as high as that reported for TiO2 films grown by CVD.  Potential-dependent capacitance measurements show that after the extn. of lithium ions has taken place a small region at the surface of the electrode has a much higher dielec. const. than that of the bulk of the electrode.  This is explained by the presence of irreversibly trapped lithium ions in the region where a (reversible) phase transformation from anatase TiO2 to anatase Li0.5TiO2 occurred.  The extent of this region depends strongly on the intercalation potential; values of 7 and 17 nm are found after 2.5 h of intercalation at -1.0 and -1.2 V vs. SCE, resp.  The dielec. const. of the modified surface region is found to range between 500 and 900.  A scheme is proposed that describes the mechanism of lithium insertion in terms of a moving TiO2|Li0.5TiO2 phase front.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrDbvBQYUKhe7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljddCzZjKKkKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK1MXkvFOgtLc%253D&amp;md5=05e415031c5f7d602462e7bc0e0a9d1a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref59"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref59" class="rightTabRefNumLink">59</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit59" data-doi="10.1039/C5TA04009D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jia, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enhanced sodium storage behavior of carbon coated anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> hollow spheres</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Carbon coated anatase TiO2 hollow spheres (CCAnTHSs) are prepd. through the carbon wrapping of etched amorphous TiO2 solid spheres (AmTSSs).  The as-obtained CCAnTHS composite is applied as an anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) for the first time, delivering excellent cycle stability.  At a high c.d. of 5C, a reversible capacity of 140.4 mA h g-1 remained after 500 cycles.  Esp., at a 25C rate it could still reach 84.9 mA h g-1 after 80 cycles.  Briefly, the sodium storage performance of CCAnTHSs are superior to these of the amorphous TiO2 solid spheres and bare anatase TiO2 hollow spheres, mainly benefitting from the advantages of a unique hollow structure with a large sp. surface area and a carbon coating with high electronic cond.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrl14AwGHxSQrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljddCzZjKKkKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtlSntLrL&amp;md5=17b0851836b51c1c08b2683391bda314</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref60"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref60" class="rightTabRefNumLink">60</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit60" data-doi="10.1021/am501772a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Oh, S.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">High-power, long-life carbon-coated TiO2 microsphere electrodes were synthesized by a hydrothermal method for sodium ion batteries, and the electrochem. properties were evaluated as a function of carbon content.  The carbon coating, introduced by sucrose addn., had an effect of suppressing the growth of the TiO2 primary crystallites during calcination.  The carbon coated TiO2 (sucrose 20 wt % coated) electrode exhibited excellent cycle retention during 50 cycles (100%) and superior rate capability up to a 30 C rate at room temp.  This cell delivered a high discharge capacity of 155 mAh gcomposite-1 at 0.1 C, 149 mAh gcomposite-1 at 1 C, and 82.7 mAh gcomposite-1 at a 10 C rate, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpxoiAXO-ILG7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljddCzZjKKkKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtVahtL7L&amp;md5=545a20e4e86c59fdac64158a8ccb76e0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref61"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref61" class="rightTabRefNumLink">61</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit61" data-doi="10.1039/C4TA05611F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ji, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jing, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hou, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Fang, L.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Banks, C.                         rightTab-cas_cit61R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">61</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon dots supported upon N-doped TiO2 nanorods applied into sodium and lithium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Yingchang; Ji, Xiaobo; Jing, Mingjun; Hou, Hongshuai; Zhu, Yirong; Fang, Laibing; Yang, Xuming; Chen, Qiyuan; Banks, Craig E.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5648-5655</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">N-doped TiO2 nanorods decorated with carbon dots with enhanced elec. cond. and faster charge transfer have been fabricated utilizing a simple hydrothermal reaction process involving TiO2 powders (P25) and NaOH in the presence of carbon dots followed by ion exchange and calcination treatments.  Due to the merits of the carbon dots, doping and nanostructures, the as-designed N-doped TiO2/C dots composite utilized as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries can sustain a capacity of 185 mA-h/g with 91.6% retention even at a high rate of 10C over 1000 cycles.  It is interesting to note that the ratios of capacitive charge capacity during such high rates for the N-doped TiO2/C dots composite electrodes are higher than those at low rates, which likely explains the obsd. excellent rate capabilities.  In contrast to lithium-ion batteries, sodium-ion batteries have gained more interest in energy storage grids because of the greater abundance and lower cost of sodium-contg. precursors.  The as-obtained N-doped TiO2/C dots composites reported here and utilized as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries exhibit excellent electrochem. performances, including substantial cycling stabilities (the capacity retention ratios after 300 cycles at 5C is 93.6%) and remarkable rate capabilities (176 mA-h/g at 5C, 131 mA-h/g at 20C); such performances are the best ever reported to date over other structured TiO2 or TiO2 composite materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpdQoz8zgNzhbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljddCzZjKKkKg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXislertb8%253D&amp;md5=59ffdc173c01a8ef1a6df7ea8b62f47c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref62"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref62" class="rightTabRefNumLink">62</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit62" data-doi="10.1039/c4ta01438c"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tian, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xin, F.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Tan, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Han, W.</span><span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High lithium electroactivity of boron-doped hierarchical rutile submicrosphere TiO<sub>2</sub></span> <span class="citation_source-journal">J.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We have reported a facile method to fabricate hierarchical boron-doped rutile submicrosphere TiO2 (SMT), whose primary particles are ∼20 nm in diam.  The as-synthesized boron-doped SMT shows excellent cycling performance and rate capability in comparison with undoped TiO2 as an anode material in Lithium-Ion Batteries (LIBs).  It has a very stable capacity of ∼190 mA h g-1 for 500 cycles at 1C.  In addn., the d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. are carried out to indicate that a low concn. (&lt;1.0 at%) of boron doping could enhance the carrier mobility μ and elec. cond. σ, and thus reveal the relationship between the electronic structure of boron-doped SMT and the performances of the boron-doped SMT anode in LIBs.  Our results also clearly demonstrate the importance and advantage of the hierarchical submicrometer-sized spherical morphol. of the TiO2 anode in LIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpckfy2h8VIX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhw8LNc1TmKOA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXhtVWrurzM&amp;md5=2db4295098114855f813a4c8939edfc0</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i12">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.6b01183" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.6b01183</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Figure showing the nitrogen absorption and desorption isotherms, EDX patterns, an initial discharge curves comparison, efficiencies in cycling at 1 C, and structure models of calculation in ball–stick styles.
